Four Male Witnesses for Zina: Proof and Responses to Objections
Proof of four male witnesses for zina and responses to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement of four male witnesses for zina, cite the Quranic proofs, and explain how the Hanafis respond to objections based on mafhum al-mukhalafa and the conflict with the debt witness verse.

Core Idea
Zina requires four male witnesses; fewer or women are not accepted. This is proven by explicit Quranic text and ijma.

The hadd punishment for zina is exceptionally severe, and so is its proof. The Quran explicitly requires four male witnesses for zina, a condition that sets it apart from other cases. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi proof for this requirement and the scholarly responses to two common objections: that Hanafis do not use mafhum al-mukhalafa, and that another verse allows two women witnesses. The resolution lies in the primacy of the prohibitive text over the permissive one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the required number and gender
The passage states: 'the condition for zina is four men'. Identify the two Quranic verses cited: [4:15] and [24:4].
Quran 24:4 also connects to the punishment for false accusation.
2
Analyze the explicit wording
The word 'أربعة' (four) is explicit (nass) regarding the number, and 'منكم' (from among you) indicates men. This is a clear dalil naqli.
Al-Binaya is cited as the source for this interpretation.
3
Examine the first objection and response
Objection: Hanafis do not use mafhum al-mukhalafa (implication of the opposite), so how can they argue that fewer than four is not allowed? Response: It is by ijma (consensus) that four are required.
The response comes from al-Zayla'i.
4
Examine the second objection and response
Objection: The verses requiring four witnesses for zina seem to conflict with [2:282] which, for financial transactions, accepts two men or one man and two women. Response: The zina verse is prohibitive (mania), the debt verse is permissive (mubiha). Prohibitive takes precedence.
The response from Fath al-Qadir is cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويقول في السرقة أخذ لا سرق) إحياء الحق المسروق منه ولا يقول سرق محافظة على الستر ولأنه لو ظهرت السرقة لوجب القطع والضمان لا يجامع القطع فلا يحصل إحياء حقه. وصرح في غاية البيان بأن قوله أخذ أولى من سرق» وعلى هذا فيحمل قول القدوري وجب أن يقول أخذ على معنى ثبت لا الوجوب الفقهي. وقوله في العناية فتعين مع قوله لا يجوز أي أن يقول سرق تسامح وإنما الكلام في الأفضل وكل منهما جائز . وحكى الفخر الرازي في التفسير أن هارون الرشيد كان . مع جماعة الفقهاء وفيهم أبو يوسف فادعى رجل على آخر بأنه أخذ ماله من بيته فأقر بالأخذ فسأل الفقهاء فأفتوا بقطع يده فقال أبو يوسف : لا لأنه لم يقر بالسرقة وإنما أقر بالأخذ فادعى المدعي أنه سرق فأقر بها فأفتوا بالقطع وخالفهم أبو يوسف فقالوا له: لم؟ قال : لأنه لما أقر أولاً بالأخذ وثبت الضمان عليه وسقط القطع فلا يقبل إقراره بعده بما يسقط الضمان عند فعجبوا ا هـ . قوله : (وشرط للزنا أربعة رجال) لقوله تعالى واللاتي يأتين الفاحشة من نسائكم فاستشهدوا عليهن أربعة منكم [النساء : ١٥] ولقوله تعالى ثم لم يأتوا بأربعة شهداء [النور: ٤] ولفظ أربعة نص في العدد والذكورة؛ كذا في البناية وأورد أنكم لا تقولون بالمفهوم فمن أين لكم عدم جواز الأقل؟ فأجاب الزيلعي بأنه بالإجماع وأورد المعارضة بين هذه وبين قوله فاستشهدوا شهيدين [البقرة : ۲۸۲] الآية. وأجاب في فتح القدير بأنها مبيحة وتلك مانعة والتقديم . قوله : (وحكي الفخر الرازي في التفسير الخ) قال الرملي : هذا ظاهر في أنه إذا ادعى أنه أخذ مالي أو دابتي تسمع وإن لم يبين وجه الأخذ اهـ ; ذكره الغزي قوله: (وأورد المعارضة الخ) قال الرملي : عبارة فتح القدير وإن النص أوجب أربعة رجال بقوله تعالى أربعة منكم فقبول امرأتين ثلاثة مخالف لما نص عليه من العدد والمعدود، وغاية الأمر المعارضة بين عموم قوله تعالى فإن لم يكونا رجلين فرجل وامرأتان وبين هذه فتقدم هذه لأنها مانعة وتلك مبيحة اهـ. ولا يخفى عليك ما
He said: 'And in theft, he should say 'he took' not 'he stole' to revive the right of the one from whom it was taken, and he should not say 'he stole' to preserve concealment, because if theft were established, cutting would be obligatory and compensation would not be compatible with cutting, so his right would not be revived. And it was explicitly stated in Ghayat al-Bayan that his saying 'took' is better than 'stole'. Based on this, the statement of al-Quduri 'he must say 'took'' is interpreted to mean 'it is established' not legal obligation. And his statement in al-`Inaya: 'it is determined' alongside his statement 'it is not permissible' for saying 'stole' is lenient; the discussion is about which is better, and both are permissible. And al-Fakhr al-Razi narrated in his Tafsir that Harun al-Rashid was with a group of jurists, among them Abu Yusuf. A man claimed against another that he took his money from his house, and he confessed to taking. He asked the jurists, and they gave a fatwa to cut off his hand. Abu Yusuf said: No, because he did not confess to theft, only to taking. Then the claimant said that he stole, and he confessed to that, so they gave a fatwa to cut, and Abu Yusuf disagreed. They said: Why? He said: Because when he first confessed to taking, compensation was established, and cutting was dropped. His subsequent confession that would drop compensation is not accepted. They were amazed. End. He said: (And the condition for zina is four men) due to His statement: 'And those who commit the unlawful act from your women, bring four witnesses from among you' [4:15] and His statement: 'Then if they do not bring four witnesses' [24:4]. The word 'four' is explicit (nass) regarding the number and masculinity. Thus in al-Binaya. And it was objected that you [Hanafis] do not rely on mafhum al-mukhalafa, so how do you derive the impermissibility of less? Al-Zayla`i answered that it is by ijma. And an objection was raised based on the conflict between this and the verse 'bring two witnesses' [2:282]. He answered in Fath al-Qadir that it [2:282] is permissive, and this [the verses for zina] is prohibitive, and the prohibitive takes precedence. He said: (And al-Fakhr al-Razi narrated in the Tafsir...) al-Ramli said: This is apparent in that if he claims 'he took my money or my animal', it is heard even if he does not explain the manner of taking. Al-Ghazzi mentioned it. He said: (And he raised the objection...) al-Ramli said: The wording of Fath al-Qadir: The text requires four men by His saying 'four from among you'. Accepting two women [instead of a fourth man] would be three, which contradicts what is stated as number and counted. The utmost is that there is a conflict between the generality of His saying 'if they are not two men, then one man and two women' and this [verse]. This [the zina verse] takes precedence because it is prohibitive and that is permissive. End. And it is not hidden from you what [follows].
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq covers two important points on testimony. First, in theft cases, when testifying, the witness should say 'he took' (أخذ) rather than 'he stole' (سرق). The reason is to preserve concealment and to allow the victim to recover his right through compensation (damam), because if the witness says 'stole', it may lead to amputation (qat') which is incompatible with compensation. The preferred wording is 'took' (أخذ), though both are permissible. The story of Harun al-Rashid and Abu Yusuf illustrates this: a man confessed to taking but not stealing; the first confession established compensation and precluded amputation; a later confession to stealing would not be accepted to drop compensation. Second, the condition for zina testimony is four male witnesses. The proof comes from Quran [4:15] and [24:4], where the word 'four' explicitly specifies the number and that they must be men. The objection that Hanafis do not use mafhum al-mukhalafa is answered by ijma: the requirement of four is established by consensus. The apparent conflict with [2:282] which mentions two witnesses in debts is resolved by noting that the debt verse is permissive (mubiha) while the zina verse is prohibitive (man'ia), and prohibitive takes precedence.

Key Terms
نص (nass): A text that is explicit and unambiguous in its meaning, requiring no interpretation.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the scholars of the community on a legal ruling.
مبيحة (mubiha): Permissive; a ruling that allows something.
مانعة (mania): Prohibitive; a ruling that forbids or restricts something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the condition for witnesses in a zina case?
A) Four witnesses, two men and two women.
B) Four male witnesses.
C) Two male witnesses or one man and two women.
D) Three male witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Zina requires four male witnesses, based on explicit Quranic text and ijma; the prohibitive nature of the ruling overrides general permissive texts.

When Testimony Becomes Obligatory: The Public vs. Private Sin Distinction
Ruling on the obligation of testimony when sin is public and recommendation of concealment when sin is hidden

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving testimony for public versus private sins and explain the Shari'ah objective that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
Testimony is wajib for public sin to eradicate evil through hudud; mustaḥabb for private sin to encourage repentance.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqāʾiq addresses a key question for witnesses: when must you step forward, and when should you stay silent? The answer hinges on the state of the sinner. If sin is flaunted, the Sharīʿah's purpose of eradicating evil (ikhlāʾ al-arḍ) demands testimony to enable punishment. If sin is hidden with remorse, concealment is recommended, following the Prophet’s ﷺ instruction to Hazzal regarding Māʿiz. This ruling also draws a subtle parallel to backbiting (ghība), illustrating the careful balance in Islamic law between exposing wrongdoing and preserving dignity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between public/open sin (where the sinner boasts and shows no shame) and private/hidden sin (where the sinner acts in secrecy with fear and regret).
The passage uses 'shuhra' for notoriety and 'mustatir' for hidden.
2
Recognize the Shari'ah objective
The Lawgiver's aim is 'ikhlāʾ al-arḍ min al-maʿāṣī' – clearing the earth of sins. This is achieved through two means: repentance (tawba) of the sinner and deterrence (zajr) through punishments.
The commentator derives the ruling from this higher objective.
3
Apply the balance
When sin is public, the likelihood of repentance is low, so the other means (hudud) becomes necessary. Thus testimony becomes obligatory (wājib) to enable the punishment. When sin is private and the sinner shows remorse, repentance is more likely, so concealment is recommended (mustaḥabb).
The Prophet's hadith to Hazzal supports the recommendation of concealment.
4
Understand the analogy to backbiting
Giving testimony in this context is likened to backbiting (ghība): what is forbidden in backbiting (speaking ill without valid reason) is forbidden here, and what is permissible (for a legitimate purpose) is permissible. Testifying against a public sinner is like permissible backbiting for the purpose of reforming or warning.
This analogy helps delineate the ethical boundaries of testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحال إلى إشاعته والتهتك به بل بعضهم ربما افتخر به فيجب كون الشهادة أولى من تركها لأن مطلوب الشارع إخلاء الأرض من المعاصي والفواحش بالخطابات المفيدة لذلك، وذلك يتحقق بالتوبة من الغافلين وبالزجر لهم فإذا ظهر حال الشهرة في الزنا مثلاً والشرب وعدم المبالاة به وإشاعته فإخلاء الأرض المطلوب حينئذ بالتوبة احتمال يقابله ظهور عدمها ممن اتصف بذلك فيجب تحقيق السبب الآخر للإخلاء وهو الحدود خلاف من زنى مرة أو مراراً مستتراً متخوفاً متندماً عليه فإنه محل استحباب ستر الشاهد وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لهزال في ماعز لو كنت سترته بثوبك الحديث وسيأتي كان في مثل من ذكرنا. وعلى هذا ذكره في غيره مجلس القاضي، وأداء الشهادة بمنزلة الغيبة فيه فيحرم منه ما يحرم منها ويحل منه ما يحل منها ا هـ . ذلك
The condition [of the situation] leading to its publicizing and exposure [of sin], indeed some may even boast of it, so giving testimony becomes more preferable than abandoning it because the Lawgiver's objective is to clear the earth of sins and immoralities through the beneficial addresses, and that is achieved by the repentance of the heedless and by deterring them. So when the situation is one of notoriety in, for example, fornication, drinking, and disregard of it and its dissemination, then the required clearing of the earth through repentance is a possibility that is counterbalanced by the apparent absence of it from one so characterized, so the other means of clearing must be realized, which is the [hadd] punishments. Contrary to one who fornicates once or repeatedly in secrecy, with fear and regret, for that is a place where it is recommended for the witness to conceal, and his statement, peace be upon him, to Hazzal regarding Ma'iz: 'If you had concealed him with your cloak,' the hadith, and it will come for one like we mentioned. And based on this, it is mentioned about other than him in the court of the judge, and giving testimony is akin to backbiting in this: so whatever is forbidden in backbiting is forbidden here, and whatever is permissible in it is permissible here. End. That.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on when testimony becomes obligatory versus recommended. The commentator explains that the Sharīʿah aims to eradicate sin from the earth. This is achieved through repentance (tawba) of the sinners and through deterrence (zajr) via punishments. When sin is committed openly and shamelessly (shuhra), the likelihood of repentance is low, so the other means—hudud penalties—must be enforced. Hence giving testimony becomes obligatory (wājib). In contrast, if the sin is done secretly with fear and regret, it is recommended (mustaḥabb) for the witness to conceal it, as the Prophet ﷺ told Hazzal regarding Ma'iz. The passage also makes an important point: giving testimony in such cases is analogous to backbiting (ghība). What is forbidden in backbiting—like speaking ill of a Muslim without valid reason—is forbidden here, and what is permissible there (e.g., for a legitimate purpose) is permissible here. This ruling is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqāʾiq and reflects the Hanafi position on the priority of concealment in private sins and exposure in public ones.

Key Terms
الشهادة (al-shahāda): Testimony in court, giving witness to a crime
الغيبة (al-ghība): Backbiting; speaking about a Muslim in his absence that he would dislike
إخلاء الأرض (ikhlāʾ al-arḍ): Clearing the earth from sin; the objective of Sharīʿah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is testifying against a fornicator obligatory rather than recommended?
A) When the fornicator is wealthy
B) When the fornication was committed in secret with regret
C) When the fornication is public and open, with no shame
D) When the witness is a close relative

Key Takeaway
The Sharīʿah balances eradicating public evil through punishment and preserving private repentance through concealment.

Choosing Wording in Theft Testimony: 'He Took' vs 'He Stole'
Ruling on preferred wording in theft testimony to avoid hadd while securing property recovery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prefers the wording 'akhadha' over 'saraqa' in theft testimony and identify the legal consequence of each phrase.

Core Idea
Using 'took' preserves the owner's right to recover property; 'stole' triggers amputation, which cancels financial liability.

In Hanafi legal procedure, the choice of words in a witness's testimony can determine the outcome of a theft case. This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq discusses whether a witness should say 'he took' (akhadha) or 'he stole' (saraqa). The preference is not a mere linguistic formality—it reflects a critical legal distinction between financial guarantee (ḍamān) and the fixed punishment of amputation (qaṭʿ). Let us examine the reasoning behind this preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Wordings
Recognize that the passage contrasts two possible testimonial phrases: 'akhadha' (took) and 'saraqa' (stole). Each triggers different legal consequences.
The Arabic terms are key: أخذ vs سرق.
2
Understand the Legal Effect of Each Phrase
If a witness says 'akhadha', only financial guarantee (ḍamān) is established, allowing the plaintiff to recover property. If he says 'saraqa', both amputation (qaṭʿ) and guarantee are necessary, but they cannot coexist in Hanafi law.
The incompatibility rule: al-qaṭʿ wa al-ḍamān lā yajtamiʿān.
3
Apply the Incompatibility Principle
Amputation removes the obligation to return the property. Therefore, using 'saraqa' would prevent the owner from recovering his goods. To protect the owner's right, 'akhadha' is preferred.
This is a practical application of the maxim that hadd and financial liability are mutually exclusive.
4
Recognize the Nature of the Ruling
The passage clarifies that saying 'akhadha' is afḍal (better) but not obligatory. Al-Qudūrī's phrase 'must say' is interpreted as 'it is established' (thabata), not as a legal obligation (wujūb). Both phrasings are permissible.
The distinction between thubūt and wujūb is a common usul point.
5
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The anecdote about Hārūn al-Rashīd and Abū Yūsuf shows that if a person first admits to 'taking' (establishing guarantee) and later admits to 'stealing', the second admission is not accepted. Abū Yūsuf's reasoning is that the first admission already fixed the guarantee and dropped the hadd.
Other jurists initially disagreed, but Abū Yūsuf's view is followed in this commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويقول في السرقة أخذ لا سرق) إحياء الحق المسروق منه ولا يقول سرق محافظة على الستر ولأنه لو ظهرت السرقة لوجب القطع والضمان لا يجامع القطع فلا يحصل إحياء حقه. وصرح في غاية البيان بأن قوله أخذ أولى من سرق» وعلى هذا فيحمل قول القدوري وجب أن يقول أخذ على معنى ثبت لا الوجوب الفقهي. وقوله في العناية فتعين مع قوله لا يجوز أي أن يقول سرق تسامح وإنما الكلام في الأفضل وكل منهما جائز . وحكى الفخر الرازي في التفسير أن هارون الرشيد كان . مع جماعة الفقهاء وفيهم أبو يوسف فادعى رجل على آخر بأنه أخذ ماله من بيته فأقر بالأخذ فسأل الفقهاء فأفتوا بقطع يده فقال أبو يوسف : لا لأنه لم يقر بالسرقة وإنما أقر بالأخذ فادعى المدعي أنه سرق فأقر بها فأفتوا بالقطع وخالفهم أبو يوسف فقالوا له: لم؟ قال : لأنه لما أقر أولاً بالأخذ وثبت الضمان عليه وسقط القطع فلا يقبل إقراره بعده بما يسقط الضمان عند فعجبوا ا هـ
His statement: (And he should say in theft: 'he took,' not 'he stole'): [Means] to revive the right of the one from whom the property was taken, and he should not say 'he stole' in order to maintain concealment, because if theft were established, then amputation and guarantee would be necessary, but guarantee and amputation do not coexist, so his right would not be revived. And it is explicitly stated in Ghāyat al-Bayān that his saying 'he took' is preferable to 'he stole.' Based on this, the statement of al-Qudūrī 'he must say "he took"' is interpreted to mean 'it is established,' not the juristic obligation. And his statement in al-ʿInāya 'so it is determined' along with his statement 'it is not permissible,' meaning that saying 'he stole' is leniency, and the discussion is about what is best, and each is permissible. And al-Fakhr al-Rāzī narrated in the Tafsīr that Hārūn al-Rashīd was with a group of jurists, among them Abū Yūsuf. A man claimed against another that he had taken his property from his house, and he admitted the taking. He asked the jurists, and they gave the fatwa to cut off his hand. Abū Yūsuf said: No, because he did not admit to theft; he only admitted to taking. Then the claimant said that he had stolen, and he admitted [to theft]. They gave the fatwa to cut off [his hand], and Abū Yūsuf disagreed. They said to him: Why? He said: Because when he first admitted to taking, the guarantee was established and the amputation dropped, so his subsequent admission that would void the guarantee is not accepted. And they were amazed. End.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the preferable wording in a theft case. The commentator explains that a witness should say 'he took (akhadha)' rather than 'he stole (saraqa)' to preserve the possibility of the plaintiff recovering his property without imposing the hadd of amputation. If the word 'stole' is used and proven, the thief’s hand is cut off, but amputation and financial liability (ḍamān) are incompatible in Hanafi law—amputation removes the obligation to return the property. To allow the owner to get his property back, the milder term 'took' is used, which establishes liability for return (ḍamān) but not the hadd. The passage notes that al-Qudūrī’s wording 'he must say' is interpreted as 'it is established' (thabata), not as a legal obligation (wujūb fiqhī). Both phrasings are permissible, but 'took' is preferable. The anecdote about Hārūn al-Rashīd and Abū Yūsuf illustrates a key nuance: if someone first admits to 'taking' (which imposes only financial guarantee), then later admits to 'stealing' (which would impose amputation but nullify guarantee), the second admission is not accepted because it would contradict the established ruling of guarantee. This is an important intra-Hanafi ruling from Abū Yūsuf.

Key Terms
القطع (al-qaṭʿ): Amputation of the hand as a hadd punishment for theft
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Financial liability; guarantee to return or compensate for the property taken
الوجوب الفقهي (al-wujūb al-fiqhī): Juristic obligation (as opposed to mere establishment of fact)
أخذ (akhadha): To take, used in testimony to imply mere taking without criminal intent
سرق (saraqa): To steal, the term that triggers hadd punishment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school prefer using 'akhadha' (took) over 'saraqa' (stole) in a theft testimony?
A) Because the word 'saraqa' is linguistically incorrect
B) To allow the owner to recover his property without imposing amputation
C) Because the word 'akhadha' is the only valid legal term
D) Because the witness might be liable for false testimony if he says 'saraqa'

Key Takeaway
The preference for 'took' over 'stole' in theft testimony is rooted in the Hanafi principle that amputation and financial guarantee cannot coexist, thus the milder term protects the victim's right to recover property.

Condition for Zina Testimony: Four Male Witnesses Required
Ruling with proof and responses to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Quranic evidence for requiring four male witnesses for zina, explain the Hanafi response to the objection based on mafhūm, and apply the rule to distinguish between permissive and restrictive texts.

Core Idea
Four male witnesses for zina are required by explicit Quranic text and ijma, not by linguistic implication.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that proving zina requires four male witnesses. We will explore the Quranic verses, the objection based on Hanafi usul not accepting mafhūm al-ʿadad, and the answers given by classical Hanafi jurists. Understanding this ruling clarifies the relationship between explicit text (nass) and consensus (ijma) in deriving conditions for hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
Read the opening sentence: 'And a condition for zina is four men.' Note the two Quranic verses cited (4:15 and 24:4). The word 'four' is textually explicit (nass) regarding number and masculinity.
The verses are: Q.4:15 and Q.24:4.
2
Understand the objection
The objection: Since Hanafis do not accept mafhūm al-ʿadad (exclusive implication of a number), why do they insist on exactly four? The objector argues that if numbers do not imply exclusivity, less than four should suffice.
Mafhūm al-ʿadad is a linguistic principle not adopted by the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Al-Zaylaʿī answers that the requirement is established by ijma (consensus) of scholars, not by linguistic implication alone. Also, the texts are explicit (nass) and thus override general usul principles.
Ijma makes the ruling binding regardless of mafhūm.
4
Distinguish between permissive and restrictive texts
Another objection: conflict with the general testimony verse (2:282) allowing two men or one man and two women. The answer from Fatḥ al-Qadīr: the zina verse is restrictive (māniʿa) and takes precedence over the permissive (mubīḥa) general verse.
Restrictive texts (al-māniʿa) are given priority over permissive ones (al-mubīḥa).
5
Apply the ruling
If less than four male witnesses are brought, the hadd for zina cannot be imposed. Instead, the accusers become liable for hadd al-qadhf (false accusation).
This is the practical consequence: a safeguard against frivolous accusations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وشرط للزنا أربعة رجال) لقوله تعالى واللاتي يأتين الفاحشة من نسائكم فاستشهدوا عليهن أربعة منكم [النساء : ١٥] ولقوله تعالى ثم لم يأتوا بأربعة شهداء [النور: ٤] ولفظ أربعة نص في العدد والذكورة؛ كذا في البناية وأورد أنكم لا تقولون بالمفهوم فمن أين لكم عدم جواز الأقل؟ فأجاب الزيلعي بأنه بالإجماع وأورد المعارضة بين هذه وبين قوله فاستشهدوا شهيدين [البقرة : ۲۸۲] الآية. وأجاب في فتح القدير بأنها مبيحة وتلك مانعة والتقديم قوله : (وحكي الفخر الرازي في التفسير الخ) قال الرملي : هذا ظاهر في أنه إذا ادعى أنه أخذ مالي أو دابتي تسمع وإن لم يبين وجه الأخذ اهـ ؛ ذكره الغزي قوله: (وأورد المعارضة الخ) قال الرملي : عبارة فتح القدير وإن النص أوجب أربعة رجال بقوله تعالى أربعة منكم فقبول امرأتين ثلاثة مخالف لما نص عليه من العدد والمعدود، وغاية الأمر المعارضة بين عموم قوله تعالى فإن لم يكونا رجلين فرجل وامرأتان وبين هذه فتقدم هذه لأنها مانعة وتلك مبيحة اهـ. ولا يخفى عليك ما
His statement: (And a condition for zina is four men) due to His statement, the Most High: 'And those who commit the indecency from your women, bring four witnesses from among you' [4:15], and His statement, the Most High: 'Then they did not bring four witnesses' [24:4]. And the word 'four' is textually explicit regarding the number and masculinity; likewise in al-Bināya. And it was objected: You [Hanafis] do not accept [the principle of] mafhūm [exclusive implication], so from where do you get the impermissibility of less? So al-Zaylaʿī answered that it is by ijma. And he raised a conflict between this [verse] and His statement 'And bring two witnesses' [2:282]. And he answered in Fatḥ al-Qadīr that this [latter] is permissive (mubīḥa) and that [the earlier] is restrictive (māniʿa) and precedence is given [to the restrictive]. His statement: (And al-Fakhr al-Rāzī narrated in the Tafsīr...) al-Ramlī said: This is apparent that if he claims 'he took my property' or 'my animal,' it is heard even if he does not explain the manner of taking. End.; al-Ghazzī mentioned it. His statement: (And he raised the conflict...) al-Ramlī said: The wording of Fatḥ al-Qadīr: Indeed the text obligated four men by His statement 'four from among you,' so accepting two women and one man is contrary to what the text stipulates in number and counted. The most that can be said is the conflict between the generality of His statement 'if there are not two men, then one man and two women' and this [verse], so this takes precedence because it is restrictive and that is permissive. End. And it is not hidden from you what [remains].
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the condition for establishing zina: four male witnesses. The commentator cites two Quranic verses (4:15 and 24:4) which explicitly mention 'four' and 'men' (min-kum implying males). He notes that in al-Bināya, the word 'four' is clear in number and gender. A common objection is raised: since Hanafis do not operate on the principle of mafhūm al-ʿadad (the exclusive implication of a number)—i.e., that mentioning a specific number automatically excludes any other number—how can they argue that less than four is impermissible? Al-Zaylaʿī answers that this ruling is established by ijma (consensus), not by linguistic implication. Another objection concerns an apparent conflict with the general verse on testimony (2:282) which allows two men or one man and two women. The commentator answers via Fatḥ al-Qadīr: the zina verse is restrictive (māniʿa) and thus takes precedence over the permissive (mubīḥa) general verse. The note about al-Fakhr al-Rāzī is a marginal comment on a different issue (theft testimony). The passage also mentions that according to al-Ramlī, the objection about linguistic implication is resolved because the text is explicit (nass) in requiring four males, so any alternative would violate the explicit count and gender.

Key Terms
المفهوم (al-mafhūm): The exclusive implication of a number (mafhūm al-ʿadad) — that the mention of a specific number excludes all others
الإجماع (al-ijmāʿ): Consensus of scholars; here used as the proof for requiring four witnesses
المبيحة (al-mubīḥa): A permissive ruling (making something mubāḥ)
المانعة (al-māniʿa): A restrictive ruling that prevents something

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi school justify the requirement of four male witnesses for zina despite not generally accepting the principle of mafhūm al-ʿadad?
A) By qiyas on theft cases
B) By citing the consensus (ijma) of scholars on this point
C) By interpreting 'four men' metaphorically
D) By arguing that the Quranic text is general and includes women

Key Takeaway
The requirement of four male witnesses for zina is a condition (shart) derived from explicit Quranic texts and unanimous scholarly consensus, not from linguistic implication.

Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
15 min
Two Male Witnesses Required for Hudud and Qisas
Ruling with proof: two men required, women excluded in hudud/qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony in hudud and qisas, identify the primary proof (hadith of al-Zuhrī), and explain the exception for financial matters.

Core Idea
Women's testimony is rejected in hudud and qisas because these punishments are warded off by doubts, and women's testimony carries a shade of substitution.

This lesson examines a core rule in Hanafi evidence law: for fixed punishments (hudud) and retaliatory penalties (qisas), only two male witnesses suffice. Women's testimony is not accepted, even alongside a man. Yet the same passage shows thoughtful exceptions: when the outcome is financial, one man and two women can testify. We will unpack the proof and the reasoning behind this nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The matn states: 'And for the remaining hudud and qisas, [the requirement is] two men.' Identify that the act is giving testimony (shahada) in hudud and qisas cases, and the ruling is that two male witnesses are a condition (shart) for proof.
The phrase 'remaining hudud' excludes zina, which has its own rule (four men).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary proof cited: the hadith of al-Zuhrī: 'The sunna continued from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ and the two khalīfas after him that there is no testimony of women in hudud and qisas.' Also note the reference to the Quranic verse (2:282) which is interpreted restrictively.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala) for Hanafis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? It explicitly states that women's testimony is not accepted in these categories. The commentator also adds a rational argument: hudud are repelled by doubts (shubahat), and women's testimony involves shubhat al-badaliya (the quality of being a substitute for men's testimony), which introduces doubt.
The substitute argument is refined: it is not actual substitution because men's testimony is possible; rather it is a resemblance that still creates doubt.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds that in specific cases (conditional manumission for drinking or theft), one man and two women can testify to establish the financial consequence (freedom or liability) but not the hadd. Other scholars might disagree. The general ruling (two men) is agreed upon, but the exception for financial outcomes is a point of intra-Hanafi variance.
The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf in these two issues, as per al-Khaniyya.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The text states: 'The fatwa in both is according to the statement of Abu Yūsuf.' The commentator also affirms that testimony of one man and two women is accepted in all matters where the outcome is financial, such as homicide by mistake or secondary testimony.
This shows that the school does not reject women's testimony entirely; it only excludes them from cases where a hadd or qisas penalty is at stake.

تجدوا ا هـ . وفي الولوالجية : رجل قال إن شربت الخمر فمملوكي حر فشهد رجل وامرأتان أنه شرب الخمر عتق العبد ولا يحد لأن هذه شهادة لا مجال لها في الحدود، ولو قال إن سرقت من فلان شيئاً فعلى قياس ما ذكرنا شهادتهم، وكذا الشهادة على الشهادة وكتاب القاضي إلى القاضي لأن هذه الجناية المال فقبل فيه شهادة الرجال مع النساء .اهـ. أقول : علم به قبول شهادة رجل وامرأتين في طرف الرجل والمرأة والحر والعبد وكل ما لا And for the remaining hudud and qisas, [the requirement is] two men. And for childbirth, virginity, and women's defects in what cannot be seen by others, due to the preventer. And we have previously mentioned in the hudud that it is permissible for the husband to be one of them except in two cases: that the husband first accuses her [of zina] and then three testify; and that he testify with them against her zina with his son willingly. Then know that manumission conditional upon zina occurs with the testimony of two men even if the master is not flogged, and the master is asked to take an oath if he denies it for the purpose of freedom, and there is a difference of opinion about this mentioned in al-Khāniyya and Adab al-Qaḍāʾ by al-Khaṣṣāf: Know that the son of her husband can be among the four. The gist of what is mentioned in al-Muḥīṭ al-Burhānī is that a man has two wives and one of them has five sons; if four of them testify against their brother that he committed zina with their father's wife, it is accepted unless the father is the claimant or their mother is alive. His statement: (And for the remaining hudud and qisas, two men) i.e., and the condition for them is the testimony of two men due to His statement, the Most High: 'And bring witnesses' etc. So the testimony of women is not accepted in them according to the hadith of al-Zuhrī: 'The sunna continued from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ and the two khalīfas after him that there is no testimony of women in hudud and qisas.' And because there is a resemblance of substitution (shubhat al-badalīya) since they stand in place of the testimony of men, so they are not accepted in matters that are warded off by doubts. Thus in al-Hidāya. And it is not actual substitution because that would be where it is impossible to act with the substitute while the original is possible, but it is not so; rather it is permissible while it is possible to act with the testimony of two men. Thus in al-ʿInāya and others. And in Khizānat al-Akmal: If a judge judged based on the testimony of one man and two women in hudud and qisas, whether he holds that view or not, then the case is raised to another judge, he should enforce it. End. And the meaning of the verse according to what the commentator mentioned: 'If they are not two men, then one man and two women' — if it were not for this interpretation, their testimony would not be considered when there are men [available]. And their testimony is considered with them when mixed with men; even if men and women testify to something, the ruling is attributed to all, so that they are all liable for guarantee when they retract. End. And al-Biqāʿī mentioned in al-Munāsabāt, attributing to al-Ḥarrānī: In the generality of the meaning of 'being' is an indication of the possibility of the testimony of two women while it is possible to request a man in some way, since He did not say 'if you do not find [men].' End. And in al-Walwālijiyya: A man said 'If I drink wine, my slave is free'; and one man and two women testified that he drank wine; the slave is freed and he is not flogged because this testimony has no scope in hudud. And if he said 'If I steal something from so-and-so,' by analogy to what we have mentioned, it should be that he is liable for the property and the slave is freed and he is not amputated. End. And he attributed the two issues in al-Khāniyya to Abū Yūsuf, then said: The fatwa in both is according to the statement of Abū Yūsuf. And in Khizānat al-Akmal regarding the issue of theft: I hold him liable and do not set free. And in [text unclear]. Contemplate. The statement of the author: 'And for the remaining hudud and qisas, two men.' Al-Ramlī said: He [the author] made it absolute, so it includes qisas for life and limb. And in al-Khāniyya: If one man and two women testify to a homicide by mistake or one that does not entail qisas, their testimony is accepted. Likewise, secondary testimony (shahāda ʿalā al-shahāda) and the letter of a judge to a judge, because this [type of] tort is [based on] property, so the testimony of men with women is accepted in it. End. I [the commentator] say: From this it is known that the testimony of one man and two women is accepted in matters concerning a man or a woman, free or slave, and everything where qisas is not required and its obligatory consequence is financial compensation; and many current cases are known from this.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a ruling with proof on the condition for testimony in hudud and qisas besides zina. The matn states two men are required. The commentator elaborates: women's testimony is not accepted in hudud and qisas based on the sunna (hadith of al-Zuhrī) and because these punishments are warded off by doubts (shubahāt). Women's testimony has a quality of substitution (badalīya), and since hudud are repelled by doubts, this substitute is not accepted. However, it is not actual substitution because it would only be if the original (two men) were impossible; here men are available. The commentator then discusses exceptions: for physical matters like childbirth, virginity, and women's defects, where women's testimony alone may be accepted due to necessity. Also, in certain cases, the husband can be one of the witnesses for his wife's zina, with exceptions. The passage then addresses conditional manumission (ʿitq muʿallaq): if a man says 'if I drink wine, my slave is free,' and one man and two women testify that he drank, the slave is freed but he is not punished with the hadd for drinking, because the testimony is not valid for hadd but is valid for financial rights. Similarly for a conditional theft. These rulings are attributed to Abū Yūsuf and the fatwa is according to him. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the phrase 'the remaining hudud and qisas' includes qisas for life and limb. But in cases where the tort is purely financial (like manslaughter by mistake), one man and two women can testify because it is a property matter. The passage concludes by stating that the accepted position is that one man and two women can testify in any matter that does not involve qisas and its result is financial compensation.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-ḥudūd): Fixed punishments ordained by God, e.g., for zina, theft, drinking.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment for homicide or bodily harm.
شبهة البدلية (shubhat al-badalīya): The quality of being a substitute; women's testimony is considered a substitute for men's testimony, introducing doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary proof that women's testimony is not accepted in hudud and qisas?
A) Quran 4:15 (the zina verse)
B) The hadith of al-Zuhri: 'The sunna continued... that there is no testimony of women in hudud and qisas'
C) Quran 2:282 (the debt verse)
D) Consensus (ijma) of the Companions

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, hudud and qisas require two male witnesses; women's testimony is excluded due to the hadith and the principle that hudud are repelled by doubts, but it is accepted in financial consequences and property-based claims.

Shahadat — Testimony

Women's Sole Testimony in Hidden Female Matters
Explaining the rule and proof for one woman's testimony in hidden female matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that one woman's testimony suffices for childbirth, virginity, and hidden defects, and explain the hadith proof and the disagreement with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
One woman's testimony proves hidden female matters; the hadith's plural includes the singular.

In certain matters that men cannot normally see, such as childbirth, virginity, and hidden genital defects, the Hanafi school accepts the testimony of a single woman. This ruling is based on a specific hadith and distinguishes the school from al-Shafi'i, who requires four women. In this lesson, we will unpack the proof, the applications, and the intra-Hanafi variations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what legal act is being ruled upon (testimony in hidden female matters) and the ruling (accepted, one woman suffices).
2
Locate and analyse the dalil
Find the hadith cited and examine how the plural 'women' is interpreted to mean the minimum of one.
The hadith: 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters that men cannot look at.'
3
Trace the application to specific cases
See how the ruling applies to childbirth, virginity, and hidden defects, including the oath of the seller in sale disputes.
Details from al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah on the oath.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Compare Abu Hanifa's view on the newborn's cry for inheritance (not accepted) with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (accepted).
Also the later position on defects before possession.
5
Engage the inter-madhhab dispute
State the Shafi'i position (requires four women), the Hanafi response (plural includes singular, ease), and the practical difference.
Thamarah al-khilaf: easier proof in Hanafi.

قوله: (وللولادة والبكارة وعيوب النساء فيما لا يطلع عليه رجل امرأة) أي وشرط امرأة أي شهادتها للحديث «شهادة النساء جائزة فيما لا يستطيع الرجال النظر إليه» والجمع المحلى بالألف واللام يراد به الجنس فيتناول الأقل وهو الواحد وهو حجة على الشافعي في اشتراط الأربع، ولأنه إنما سقط الذكورة ليخف النظر لأن نظر الجنس أخف فكذا يسقط اعتبار العدد لأن المثنى والثلاث أحوط لما فيه من معنى الإلزام، ثم حكمها في الولادة شرحناه في باب ثبوت النسب، وفي البكارة شرحناه في باب العنين من أنهن إن شهدن ببكارتها يؤجل العنين سنة ويفرق بعده لأنها تأيدت بمؤيد إذ البكارة أصل، وكذا في رد البيع إذا اشتراها بشرط البكارة وإن قلن إنها ثيب يحلف البائع لينضم نكوله إلى قولهن والعيب يثبت بقولهن فيحلف البائع؛ كذا في الهداية. وأورد عليه أنه لو ثبت العيب بقولهن لم يحلف البائع بل نرد عليه الجارية فكيف يكون تحليف البائع نتيجة لثبوت العيب في الجارية، بل ثبوت العيب بقولهن يثبت الرد لا التحليف وأجاب عنه في النهاية بأن ثبوته بقولهن لسماع الدعوى وفي حق التحليف إذ لولا شهادتهن لم يحلف البائع وكان القول له بلا يمين لتمسكه بالأصل وهو البكارة ا هـ . وظاهر اقتصاره على الثلاثة يفيد أن قول المرأة بل النساء لا يقبل في غيرها ولكن في خزانة الأكمل : لو شهد عنده نسوة عدول أنها امرأة فلان أو ابنته وسعته الشهادة ا هـ. وفيها يقبل تعديل المرأة ولا تقبل ترجمتها وأطلق في الولادة ويستثنى منه الشهادة على استهلال الصبي في حق الإرث عند أبي حنيفة لأنه مما يطلع عليه الرجال، ويمكن أن يخرج من كلام المصنف بقوله فيما لا يطلع عليه رجل إن كان قيداً في الكل وإن كان الظاهر رجوعه إلى الأخير. وأما في حق الصلاة فتقبل شهادتها اتفاقاً لأنها الدين، وعندهما تقبل في حق الإرث أيضاً وبقولهما قال الشافعي وأحمد وهو أرجح؛ كذا في من أمور قوله : (أي وشرط امرأة أي شهادتها قال الرملي : سيأتي قريباً أن قبول شهادتها الثبوت سماع الدعوى لا لثبوت الرد بها فأفهم والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم. قال في الخانية: وفيما لا ينظر إليه الرجال كالقرن والرتق ونحوه اختلفت الروايات وآخر ما روي عن محمد أنه إن كان قبل القبض وهو عيب لا يحدث ترد بشهادة النساء وهو قول أبي يوسف الآخر، والمرأة الواحدة والمرأتان سواء والمرأتان أوثق . وأما الحبل فيثبت بقول النساء في حق الخصومة ولا ترد بشهادتهن قوله : (وظاهر اقتصاره على الثلاثة (الخ قال الرملي : ذكر في الدرر والغرر والولادة واستهلال الصبي للصلاة عليه كذا فتح القدير، وتقدمت في باب ثبوت النسب. وأشار بقوله فيما لا يطلع عليه رجل» إلى أن الرجل لو شهد لا تقبل شهادته وهو محمول على ما إذا قال تعمدت النظر، أما إذا شهد بالولادة وقال فاجأتها فاتفق نظري عليها تقبل شهادته إذا كان عدلاً كما في المبسوط. وفي خزانة الأكمل : ولا تقبل شهادة الكافرة والمملوكة وإنما تقبل شهادة الحرة المسلمة.
His saying: (And for childbirth, virginity, and defects of women in what a man cannot see: one woman) i.e., a woman is a condition, i.e., her testimony, due to the hadith: 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters that men cannot look at.' The plural with alif and lam indicates the genus, so it includes the least, which is one. This is an argument against al-Shafi'i in requiring four. And because masculinity was dropped only to lighten looking, since looking at the gender is lighter, so likewise the consideration of number is dropped, because two or three are more cautious due to the sense of obligation. Then its ruling in childbirth we explained in the chapter of proving lineage, and in virginity we explained in the chapter of the impotent husband: that if they testify to her virginity, the impotent husband is given a year and then separated, because it is supported by a support since virginity is the original state. Likewise in returning a sale if he bought her with the condition of virginity; if they say she is deflowered, the seller is made to take an oath so that his refusal supports their statement, and the defect is proven by their statement, so the seller is made to take an oath; as in al-Hidayah. An objection was raised that if the defect is proven by their statement, the seller should not be made to take an oath; rather we return the slave girl; so how is making the seller take an oath a result of proving the defect in the slave girl? Rather proving the defect by their statement establishes return, not the oath. He answered in al-Nihayah that its establishment by their statement is for hearing the claim and regarding the oath, because were it not for their testimony, the seller would not have been made to take an oath, and the statement would be his without an oath due to his adherence to the original state, which is virginity. End. The apparent restriction to three indicates that the statement of a woman, or of women, is not accepted in other matters, but in Khizanat al-Akmal: if righteous women testify before him that she is the wife of so-and-so or his daughter, their testimony is permissible. End. In it, the tazkiyah (validation) of a woman is accepted, but her translation (interpretation) is not accepted. He gave an absolute in childbirth, but an exception is made for testimony on the crying of a newborn concerning inheritance according to Abu Hanifa, because it is something men can see. It can be extracted from the author's statement 'in what a man cannot see' if it is a restriction for all, though apparently it refers to the last. As for the prayer, her testimony is accepted by agreement because it is religion. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is accepted regarding inheritance as well, and their statement is followed by al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, and it is more preponderant; as in... His saying: (i.e., a woman is a condition, i.e., her testimony) al-Ramli said: It will come shortly that acceptance of her testimony is for establishing the hearing of the claim, not for establishing the return by it, so understand. And Allah knows best. He said in al-Khaniyah: Regarding what men cannot see, like the clitoris, atresia, etc., the narrations differed, and the latest from Muhammad is that if it was before taking possession and it is a defect that does not arise, the slave girl is returned by women's testimony; that is the other statement of Abu Yusuf. One woman and two women are equal, but two women are more trustworthy. As for pregnancy, it is established by women's statement regarding litigation, and the slave girl is not returned by their testimony. His saying: (And apparent restriction to three) al-Ramli said: He mentioned in al-Durar wa al-Ghurar that childbirth and the crying of a newborn for praying over it are as in Fath al-Qadir, and it preceded in the chapter of proving lineage. And he indicated by his saying 'in what a man cannot see' that if a man testifies, his testimony is not accepted, and this is interpreted as when he says he intentionally looked. But if he testifies to childbirth and says 'I came upon her suddenly and my sight happened upon her,' his testimony is accepted if he is upright, as in al-Mabsut. In Khizanat al-Akmal: The testimony of a disbeliever or a slave woman is not accepted; only the testimony of a free Muslim woman is accepted.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's rule regarding women's testimony in matters that men cannot normally see, such as childbirth, virginity, and hidden defects of women (e.g., genital abnormalities). The author explains that the matn's statement 'woman' (singular) is deliberate, supported by the hadith: 'The testimony of women is permissible in matters that men cannot look at.' The plural 'women' in the hadith is interpreted as generic, including the singular minimum. This is a clear argument against Imam al-Shafi'i's requirement of four women. The rationale is that the requirement of male testimony was dropped to ease scrutiny, and likewise the number is reduced to one, though two or three are more cautious. The commentary then elaborates on applications: for virginity, if women testify that a bride is a virgin, the impotent husband is given a year's grace before separation; if they testify she is deflowered, the seller of a slave girl must swear an oath. There is a subtle discussion: if a defect is proven by women's testimony, why is the seller required to swear an oath? The answer from al-Nihayah is that the testimony is accepted to hear the claim and to shift the burden; without it, the seller's word would be taken due to the original presumption of virginity. The passage also notes exceptions: at Abu Hanifa, testimony on a newborn's cry for inheritance is not accepted (since men can see it), but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad accept it, and al-Shafi'i and Ahmad also agree. Later, it clarifies that only free Muslim women's testimony is accepted; disbelievers and slave women are excluded. The passage ends by discussing that if a man unintentionally witnesses childbirth, his testimony may be accepted if he is upright.

Key Terms
شهادة النساء (shahādat al-nisā'): The testimony given by women, which is accepted in certain matters like childbirth, virginity, and female defects that men cannot see.
الولادة (al-wilādah): Childbirth; matters related to the birth of a child that require testimony for lineage or inheritance.
البكارة (al-bakārah): Virginity; a condition of a woman that can be testified to by women for legal purposes like impotence or sale conditions.
عيوب النساء (‘uyūb al-nisā'): Hidden defects of women, such as genital abnormalities (e.g., ratq, qarn), that men cannot normally see.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many women are needed to prove a hidden defect (like atresia) in a slave girl before possession?
A) Four women
B) Two women
C) One woman is sufficient
D) Only men can testify

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the testimony of a single woman for childbirth, virginity, and hidden defects, based on the hadith that the testimony of women is permissible in matters men cannot see.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
General Rule for Women's Testimony: Two Men or One Man and Two Women
Explaining the general rule for women's testimony with men, including proof and rational justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for women's testimony in non-hidden matters and explain the Quranic and rational proofs that underpin it.

Core Idea
For most matters, testimony requires two men or one man and two women – a rule rooted in Quran and rational compensation for forgetfulness.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the default structure of testimony for non-hidden matters. The rule is derived directly from the Quranic verse on debt (2:282) and is applied broadly to property and non-property cases. The passage also includes a fascinating rational defense of women's intellectual capacity, showing that the rule is not based on deficiency in religious obligation but on practical compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
What is the required number and gender composition of witnesses? The ruling: two men OR one man and two women for all matters except those exclusively known to women. Note that four women without a man are not accepted.
The text mentions that this rule applies 'for other than that' (i.e., matters not hidden from men).
2
Trace the Quranic proof
Locate the Quranic verse (2:282) cited: 'If one of them errs, the other will remind her.' Explain how the conditional clause implies the substitution of two women for one man. The verse is the primary dalil.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah in its application to non-debt matters.
3
Examine the scope of the rule
The ruling covers property, marriage, divorce, agency, will, manumission, and lineage. Note that the Hanafi school applies it broadly, unlike some other schools that limit it to financial matters.
The author uses 'أطلقه' (he made it absolute) indicating the rule is general.
4
Understand the rationale
Why is a second woman required? Because of increased forgetfulness (ziyadat al-nisyan). This is compensated by adding another woman, not because of any inherent deficiency in women's capacity for testimony.
The text states: 'ونقصان الضبط بزيادة النسيان انجبر بضم الأخرى إليها'
5
Analyze the intellectual argument
Examine al-Akmal's four-stage intellect theory: hylic, dispositive, actual, acquired. The dispositive intellect (basis of taklif) is fully present in women. The hadith 'deficient in intellect' refers to the actual intellect, affecting leadership but not testimony.
This argument refutes the misconception that the testimony rule implies intellectual deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولغيرها رجلان أو رجل وامرأتان للآية أطلقه فشمل المال وغيره كالنكاح والطلاق والوكالة والوصية والعتاق والنسب لأن الأصل في شهادة النساء القبول لوجود ما يبتنى عليه أهلية الشهادة وهي المشاهدة والضبط والأداء ونقصان الضبط بزيادة النسيان انجبر بضم الأخرى إليها فلم يبق بعد ذلك إلا الشبهة ولهذا لا تقبل فيما يندرئ بالشبهات وهذه الحقوق تثبت بالشبهات، وإنما لا تقبل شهادة الأربع من غير رجل كيلا يكثر خروجهن، وحكي أن أم بشر شهدت عند الحاكم فقال الحاكم: فرقوا بينهما. فقالت: ليس لك ذلك قال الله تعالى إن تضل إحداهما فتذكر إحداهما الأخرى [البقرة: ٢٨٢] فسكت الحاكم؛ في الملتقط .. وقد حقق الأكمل في العناية هنا تحقيقاً حسناً كما هو دأبه فقال لا نقصان في عقلهن فيما هو مناط التكليف وبيان ذلك لأن للنفس الإنسانية أربع مراتب : الأولى استعداد العقل ويسمى العقل الهيولاني وهو حاصل لجميع أفراد الإنسان من مبدأ فطرتهم. والثانية أن يحصل البديهيات باستعمال الحواس في الجزئيات فتتهيأ لاكتساب الفكريات ويسمى العقل بالملكة وهو مناط التكليف والثالثة أن تحصل النظريات المفروغ عنها متى شاء من غير افتقار إلى اكتساب بالفكرة ويسمى العقل بالفعل والرابعة أن يستحضرها ويلتفت إليها مشاهدة ويسمى العقل المستفاد وليس فيما هو مناط التكليف منها وهو العقل بالملكة فيهن نقصان بمشاهدة حالهن في تحصيل البديهيات باستعمال الحواس في الجزئيات وبالنسبة إن ثبتت، فإنه لو كان في ذلك نقصان لكان تكليفهن دون تكليف الرجال في الأركان وليس كذلك، وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «ناقصات عقل المراد به العقل بالفعل ولذلك لم يصلحن للولاية والخلافة والإمارة ا هـ . وهكذا ذكره في آخر التوضيح ومثل الأول في التلويح بقوة الطفل على الكتابة، والثاني باستعداد الرجل الأمي للكتابة والثالث باستعداد القادر على الكتابة، والرابع بقدرته على الكتابة حالة الكتابة. وأوردت على قوله ولغيرها الشهادة بإسلام الكافر فإنه لا ه
His saying: (And for other than that, two men or a man and two women) due to the verse [2:282]. He made it absolute, so it includes property and others like marriage, divorce, agency, will, manumission, and lineage, because the principle regarding women's testimony is acceptance, due to the existence of what the capacity for testimony is based on: witnessing, retention, and performance. The deficiency in retention due to increased forgetfulness is compensated by adding another woman. After that, only suspicion remains. Hence it is not accepted in matters that are averted by suspicions, and these rights are established by suspicions. And the testimony of four without a man is not accepted so that women do not go out frequently. It is narrated that Umm Bishr testified before a judge, and the judge said: 'Separate them.' She said: 'You have no right to that; Allah said: If one of them errs, the other will remind her' [2:282]. The judge remained silent. In al-Multaqat... And al-Akmal in al-Inayah verified here a good verification, as is his habit: he said there is no deficiency in their intellect concerning what is the basis of obligation. The explanation is that the human soul has four ranks: first, the preparedness of intellect, called the hylic intellect, present in all humans from their innate nature. Second, that the self-evident truths are obtained by using the senses in particulars, so one becomes prepared to acquire intellectual truths; this is called the intellect by disposition, and it is the basis of obligation. Third, that the theoretical matters already resolved become present whenever one wishes without needing to be acquired by thought; this is called the intellect in act. Fourth, that one brings them to mind and attends to them as observed; this is called the acquired intellect. In what is the basis of obligation, which is the intellect by disposition, there is no deficiency in them, as observed from their condition in acquiring self-evident truths by using the senses in particulars, if it is established. For if there were deficiency in that, then their obligation would be less than the obligation of men in the pillars, but it is not. And the Prophet's saying 'deficient in intellect' refers to the intellect in act, and therefore they are not fit for guardianship, caliphate, or leadership. End. Thus he mentioned it at the end of al-Tawdih. And he likened the first to the strength of a child for writing, the second to the preparedness of an illiterate man for writing, the third to the preparedness of one who can write, and the fourth to his ability to write while writing. An objection was raised on his saying 'and for other than that' concerning the testimony of a disbeliever's conversion to Islam, for it is not...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage explains the general rule that for matters other than the hidden female ones mentioned earlier, the required testimony is either two men or one man with two women. The proof is the Quranic verse 2:282 (the debt verse). The author distinguishes between property and non-property matters like marriage, divorce, agency, etc., all of which fall under this rule. The rationale is that women have the basic capacity for testimony (witnessing, retention, delivery) but are prone to forgetfulness, so another woman is added to compensate. The rule is not based on inherent deficiency in intellect regarding religious obligations. A detailed rational argument from al-Akmal is presented: the human intellect has four stages – hylic, dispositive, actual, and acquired. The dispositive intellect, which is the basis of legal obligation (taklif), is fully present in women, so they are equally obligated in the fundamentals of religion. The hadith 'deficient in intellect' refers to the actual intellect, which affects their suitability for leadership roles, not for testimony. The passage also references a story where a woman (Umm Bishr) successfully cited the Quranic verse to silence a judge who tried to separate her from another female witness. This illustrates that the requirement of two women or one man and two women is from the Quran itself. The passage ends with an objection about testimony regarding conversion to Islam, which is cut off in this window.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony; legal evidence given in court. For women, it is accepted with conditions as per Quran and Sunnah.
العقل الهيولاني (al-‘aql al-hayūlānī): The hylic intellect; the innate potential for intelligence present in all humans from birth.
العقل بالملكة (al-‘aql bi al-malakah): The intellect by disposition; the ability to reason and understand self-evident truths, which is the basis for legal obligation.
العقل بالفعل (al-‘aql bi al-fi‘l): The intellect in act; the ability to actively reason about theoretical matters; women may be deficient in this, explaining the hadith about 'deficient intellect'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for requiring two women instead of one man in testimony?
A) Women have less intelligence
B) Women are more prone to forgetfulness, so another woman is added
C) Women are not allowed to testify alone
D) Quran requires two women for everything

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for women's testimony in non-hidden matters requires two men or one man and two women, derived from Quran 2:282 and supported by rational analysis that women are not deficient in the intellect needed for religious obligations.

Women's Testimony in Non-Hudud Matters: Ruling, Proof, and Intellectual Basis
Explaining the ruling and proof for women's testimony in non-hudud matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women's testimony in non-hudud matters, identify its Quranic proof, and explain the Hanafi defense of women's intellectual capacity for legal obligation.

Core Idea
Women's testimony is accepted in all non-hudud cases; forgetfulness is compensated by pairing two women.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on women's testimony in matters other than hudud and retaliation. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that women's testimony is accepted with two men or one man and two women, covering property, marriage, divorce, and more. We will explore the Quranic proof and the sophisticated Hanafi argument that women possess the necessary intellect for legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the general rule: for non-hudud matters, testimony is accepted with two men or one man and two women. Note that the rule is absolute, covering property and other matters like marriage, divorce, agency, will, manumission, and lineage.
The ruling is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and expanded in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
Cite Surah al-Baqarah 2:282 as the primary dalil. The verse establishes the principle of two women substituting for one man in testimony, indicating acceptance when there is a man.
The verse is absolute (mutlaq) and applies to all non-hudud matters.
3
Understand the rationale
Explain the three bases of testimony: observation (mushahada), retention (dabt), and performance (ada'). Deficiency in retention due to forgetfulness is compensated by adding a second woman, so the testimony becomes valid.
This rationale distinguishes Hanafi from other schools.
4
Differentiate from hudud cases
Women's testimony is not accepted in hudud because those are averted by doubts (shubuhat), whereas these non-hudud rights are established despite doubts.
The passage states 'these rights are established by doubts' meaning they are not fully certain but still actionable.
5
Analyze the intellectual defense
Follow al-Akmal's four-level classification of intellect: hylic, dispositional (al-`aql bi al-malaka), actual, and acquired. Women are not deficient in the dispositional intellect, which is the basis of taklif. The hadith 'deficient in intellect' refers to the actual intellect, not the dispositional.
This defense shows that women are fully obligated in religious duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولغيرها رجلان أو رجل وامرأتان للآية أطلقه فشمل المال وغيره كالنكاح والطلاق والوكالة والوصية والعتاق والنسب لأن الأصل في شهادة النساء القبول لوجود ما يبتنى عليه أهلية الشهادة وهي المشاهدة والضبط والأداء ونقصان الضبط بزيادة النسيان انجبر بضم الأخرى إليها فلم يبق بعد ذلك إلا الشبهة ولهذا لا تقبل فيما يندرئ بالشبهات وهذه الحقوق تثبت بالشبهات، وإنما لا تقبل شهادة الأربع من غير رجل كيلا يكثر خروجهن، وحكي أن أم بشر شهدت عند الحاكم فقال الحاكم: فرقوا بينهما. فقالت: ليس لك ذلك قال الله تعالى إن تضل إحداهما فتذكر إحداهما الأخرى [البقرة: ٢٨٢] فسكت الحاكم؛ في الملتقط .. وقد حقق الأكمل في العناية هنا تحقيقاً حسناً كما هو دأبه فقال لا نقصان في عقلهن فيما هو مناط التكليف وبيان ذلك لأن للنفس الإنسانية أربع مراتب : الأولى استعداد العقل ويسمى العقل الهيولاني وهو حاصل لجميع أفراد الإنسان من مبدأ فطرتهم. والثانية أن يحصل البديهيات باستعمال الحواس في الجزئيات فتتهيأ لاكتساب الفكريات ويسمى العقل بالملكة وهو مناط التكليف والثالثة أن تحصل النظريات المفروغ عنها متى شاء من غير افتقار إلى اكتساب بالفكرة ويسمى العقل بالفعل والرابعة أن يستحضرها ويلتفت إليها مشاهدة ويسمى العقل المستفاد وليس فيما هو مناط التكليف منها وهو العقل بالملكة فيهن نقصان بمشاهدة حالهن في تحصيل البديهيات باستعمال الحواس في الجزئيات وبالنسبة إن ثبتت، فإنه لو كان في ذلك نقصان لكان تكليفهن دون تكليف الرجال في الأركان وليس كذلك، وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «ناقصات عقل المراد به العقل بالفعل ولذلك لم يصلحن للولاية والخلافة والإمارة ا هـ. وهكذا ذكره في آخر التوضيح ومثل الأول في التلويح بقوة الطفل على الكتابة، والثاني باستعداد الرجل الأمي للكتابة والثالث باستعداد القادر على الكتابة، والرابع بقدرته على الكتابة حالة الكتابة.
His statement: (And for other than that, two men or one man and two women) due to the verse, he made it absolute so it includes property and other than it like marriage, divorce, agency, will, manumission, and lineage because the original principle regarding women's testimony is acceptance due to the existence of what the eligibility of testimony is built upon, which are observation, retention, and performance; and the deficiency in retention due to increased forgetfulness is compensated by joining another woman to her, so nothing remains except doubt, and for this it is not accepted in matters that are averted by doubts, and these rights are established by doubts. Second, to acquire intuitive knowledge by using the senses in particulars, so it becomes ready to acquire discursive knowledge, called the intellect as disposition, and this is the basis of obligation. Third, to acquire theoretical knowledge that is already concluded whenever one wishes without needing to acquire by thought, called the actual intellect. Fourth, to bring it to mind and attend to it observantly, called the acquired intellect. There is no deficiency in that which is the basis of obligation from these, which is the intellect as disposition, in them [women] by observing their condition in acquiring intuitive knowledge by using the senses in particulars, and in relation if it is established. For if there were deficiency in that, their obligation would be lesser than that of men in the pillars, and that is not so. And the Prophet's saying, 'Women are deficient in intellect' refers to the actual intellect, and therefore they are not suitable for guardianship, caliphate, and leadership. End quote. And thus it is mentioned at the end of al-Tawdih. Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, which states the general rule for women's testimony: in all matters other than hudud and retaliation (the domain where women's testimony is not accepted at all), testimony is accepted with two men or one man and two women. The author expands on this ruling, explaining that the Quranic verse (2:282) is the proof, and that the rule is absolute, covering financial matters as well as marriage, divorce, agency, will, manumission, and lineage. The reason women's testimony is accepted is that the foundational elements of testimony—observation, retention, and performance—are present in women. The deficiency in retention due to forgetfulness is compensated by having two women. Thus, only a suspicion remains, and these rights are established despite suspicion. The testimony of four women without a man is not accepted to avoid excessive public appearance of women. A story is related about Umm Bishr who cited the verse to a judge. Then al-Akmal's analysis from al-`Inayah is quoted, explaining that women are not deficient in the intellect that is the basis of legal obligation (al-`aql bi al-malaka). He distinguishes four levels of intellect: hylic, dispositional (basis of taklif), actual, and acquired. The hadith about deficiency in women's intellect refers to the actual intellect, not the dispositional one. The commentator uses analogies of a child writing and an illiterate man to illustrate these levels. The passage confirms that women's testimony is accepted on solid grounds.

Key Terms
العقل بالملكة (al-`aql bi al-malaka): The intellect as disposition: the state where one has acquired intuitive knowledge from sensory experience, ready for discursive reasoning. It is the basis of legal obligation (taklif).
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony given in court; a legal statement based on observation, retention, and performance.
النسيان (al-nisyan): Forgetfulness, considered a natural deficiency in retention that is compensated by having two women witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the testimony of four women without a man not accepted?
A) Because they are deficient in intellect
B) To avoid excessive public appearance of women
C) Because the Quran does not mention it
D) Because four women cannot remember

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts women's testimony in all non-hudud matters based on the Quranic verse, compensating forgetfulness by pairing two women, and affirming women's full intellectual capacity for legal obligation.

Testimony on Childbirth and the Effect of Intentional Looking
Ruling on testimony on childbirth with intentional looking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a man's testimony regarding childbirth and explain why intentional looking does not disqualify the witness according to some scholars.

Core Idea
A man's testimony on childbirth is accepted despite looking at awrah—necessity overrides the prohibition.

In cases of childbirth, exposure of a woman's private parts is unavoidable. The Hanafi school addresses the tension between the prohibition of looking at awrah and the need for evidence to establish lineage. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling and the difference of opinion on whether a witness's intentional looking affects his testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
Understand that the passage deals with whether a man can testify about a woman giving birth, despite the fact that he would have seen her private parts, which is normally prohibited.
2
Examine the Basis of Acceptance
The base text (matn) indicates acceptance of testimony from two men on birth. The prohibition of looking is overridden due to necessity, like in adultery witnesses.
Necessity (darurah) permits actions that would otherwise be forbidden for the sake of preserving rights.
3
Distinguish Accidental vs Intentional Looking
The passage notes that the looking might be accidental (without intent) or due to necessity. Some scholars extend acceptance even if the witness admits intentional looking.
Intentional looking does not necessarily invalidate the testimony because the need for testimony overrides the sin.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
The text cites a difference: some Hanafi scholars reject testimony if the witness intentionally looked, while others accept it. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sides with acceptance.
This ikhtilaf shows a range within the school.
5
Determine the Relied-Upon Position
The author concludes with 'fathabata' (so it is established), indicating that the ruling of acceptance is confirmed for practice.
The mufta bihi appears to be acceptance, though the issue remains debated in later texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Consider his statement: (And he indicated by his statement 'in what men do not see') ... al-Ramli said: He presented in the chapter on proving lineage in the commentary on his statement 'And the woman in idda if she denies her birth with the testimony of two men' ... that he conveyed by his statement 'with the testimony of two men' the acceptance of men's testimony regarding birth from a non-mahram woman, and that they do not become sinners by looking at her private parts, either because it may happen accidentally without intent or deliberately, or due to necessity as in witnesses of adultery. Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on a man's testimony regarding the birth of a child. The issue is that normally a man is not allowed to look at a non-mahram woman's private parts, but in the context of witnessing childbirth, the testimony is accepted. Al-Ramli explains that the text of the matn indicated that two men can testify to birth, and they are not considered sinners for looking, either because the look was accidental or due to necessity (like in adultery cases). There is a difference of opinion: some scholars accept the testimony even if the man says he intentionally looked. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq then says 'So it is established' (fathabata), indicating that the ruling is confirmed. The passage suggests that the relied-upon position is that testimony is accepted regardless of intent, though there is a later discussion on pages 105 that indicates a dispute.

Key Terms
الولادة (al-wilada): Childbirth; here specifically a woman giving birth, which is a situation where private parts must be exposed, thus requiring special ruling on testimony.
تعمد النظر (ta'ammud al-nazar): Intentional looking at a woman's private parts; if the witness admits to this, there is ikhtilaf on whether testimony is accepted.
